## Course Introduction {#course-introduction}

### What is dbt and Why should you use it? {#what-is-dbt-and-why-should-you-use-it}

```
Welcome to your DBT course.
My name is Zoltan and I'm incredibly excited to have you in this course.
I'm committed to making you a DBT expert, whether you come from an engineering background, analytics
or anywhere else.
If you have a very data, you already know the pain.
It's hundreds of SQL scripts scattered everywhere.
One change breaks free reports.
No one knows which queries are actually being used, right?
And then something goes wrong.
Good luck figuring out what happened.
Sounds familiar.
Here is a better question for you.
What if you could apply software engineering best practices like version control testing documentation
directly into your SQL transformations?
This is exactly why DBT has become the industry standard for modern data teams.
And in this course you will learn all about it.
Now what is DBT?
At its core, DBT is super, super simple.
It takes select statements and turns them into powerful production ready data transformation workflows.
Think about it this way DBT is to data transformation what git is to software development.
It brings all the best practices from software engineering like version control testing, documentation,
modularity, you name it directly into your analytics workflow.
Now, how does it do that?
Instead of writing messy one off SQL scripts, you write modular models which are basically select statements
and DBT simply handles the rest.
It figures out the dependencies, runs things in the right order, and materializes your transformations
in your data warehouse.
And here is what it makes it truly powerful.
DBT core is open source and database agnostic.
This means that everything you learn in this course applies whether you use snowflake or BigQuery or
Redshift or Databricks or any other modern data warehouse.
So you learn once and then use everywhere.
So why is DBT transforming how data teams work?
Imagine your current analytics setup.
It's usually SQL scripts scattered across folders.
Hard coded dependencies, no testing, no documentation whatsoever.
Or if there is documentation is out of date and it comes with a bunch of spaghetti SQL code.
Right now imagine rather this an organized project structure where every transformation is a modular,
tested, documented building block with reporting included.
And this is exactly what it does for you.
Here are some of its main features.
First, built in testing so you can automatically test that your data meets expectations.
For example non-null values in key columns, referential integrity between tables, custom business
logic, unit tests, data test data contracts, you name it.
This way, you can catch data quality issues before they reach your dashboard or your CFO.
I actually learned that the hard way.
So our CFO was the data quality check in one of the first companies I worked with.
And trust me, it's not the best setup you can have anyway.
So secondly, DBT has automatic documentation.
Your code generates a searchable documentation site with lineage graphs that show exactly how data flows
through your transformations.
So now we're rendering like where does this metric come from?
And there is more.
With today's DBT extensions you can even work with column level lineage.
So you will be able to see how certain values of a certain column pass through your pipeline, and there
is modularity and reusability.
You write your transformation logic once, reference it from everywhere, you change it in one place
and it will update your entire project.
And here is the best part.
DBT has become the industry standard.
Companies from startups to fortune 500 use it.
So it's not just a tool, it's an actual career skill.
Who is DBT for?
If you're a data analyst tired of fragile SQL scripts, DBT will give you engineering superpowers,
just like in data engineering.
If you're a data engineer building data pipelines, you can just think of DBT as a transformation layer
of your stack.
And if you're an analytics engineer, well, congrats!
This tool has literally defined your role in this course.
We are taking you from zero to hero.
You will implement a real world project covering every major feature and best practice, and I'm going
to share lessons learned in my project experience along the way.
And I promise by the end you won't just know DBT, you will actually think like an analytics engineer.
Let's dive right in.
```

### How can you take the most out of this course? {#how-can-you-take-the-most-out-of-this-course}

```
Let's discuss how you will get the most out of this course.
This course has been on Udemy since 2022 and I've been updating it regularly.
So in the videos you will see different versions of me.
Also for the theoretical section, Mike, a friend of mine joined me in this course.
You will meet him later once we cover the theoretical part.
Now regarding theory.
Most of you are here to get your hands dirty and get up to speed with DBT in the shortest possible time,
right?
And I keep that in mind.
Some of you, though, might want to explore more of the theoretical background on how the modern data
stack evolved and what its main concepts are to cater for both needs.
We have added a full blown theoretical module to the end of the course.
You will find it under the Reference Theory section if you scroll down in your Udemy curriculum.
If you want to explore this section, I suggest you to take some time and go through it before you proceed
to the next video.
If not, rest assured we will explain all the theory needed to complete the exercises during the lectures.
Even if you don't jump to the theory section now.
This will give you the fastest possible way to get your feet wet with DBT.
Also, as stated in the prerequisites, this course assumes that you have basic familiarity with Python
and know how to set up a virtual environment.
Would that be not the case?
We've added reference materials to help you get up to speed with these technologies.
You will find these again in the reference section at the end of the course curriculum.
What you will learn here applies to every modern data warehouse.
We will use snowflake, but if you use Databricks or any other technology, don't worry about snowflake.
What you'll learn is directly applicable everywhere.
Also, you will probably have questions throughout this course and this is great.
I would like to ask you to use the Udemy Q&amp;A feature if you have any questions.
I regularly monitor the Q&amp;A and I will respond to questions as soon as possible.
However, I cannot guarantee a timely response to private messages or LinkedIn messages.
And lastly, Udemy provides you with the option to download the course completion certificate, but
it requires you to watch all the videos to do that.
But there is a trick.
If there are sections that aren't your core interests, you can simply check the checkbox beside the
course title in the curriculum list and mark it as watched.
All right, now you know everything to get started.
Next you will see some links to resources.
Please bookmark them now we will use the course's GitHub page exclusively for code snippets, solutions
and other code material, so please keep it handy.
I'll see you in the next video where we will get ready to set up our first DBT project.
```

## Models {#models}

### Use-case and Input Data Model Overview {#use-case-and-input-data-model-overview}

```
In this course, you will act as if you were an analytics engineer in Airbnb.
And as an analytics engineer, you're responsible for all the data flow in Berlin, Germany, in Europe.
So this takes quite a lot of responsibilities.
So you will need to import your data into a data warehouse and then make this data available for tools
like DBT.
Then we will need to go and cleanse the data and also do several transformations on the data and export
it to a BI tool.
I will guide you to all of these steps in this course.
And once we have a pipeline that works, we will make sure that this pipeline is then well tested and
well organized and well documented.
The data we work with here is real world data, and the data itself comes from the inside.
Airbnb page, which is Airbnb's data sharing website.
You see here are all the Airbnb listings about Berlin, Germany.
We downloaded this data for you and prepared it to be ready to import it to snowflake.
And once we have them imported, we can get started right away.
The tech stack we are going to use here is obviously DBT, right?
And as the data warehouse we will use snowflake, which is a cloud based data warehouse that offers
a free trial.
So I will guide you through the registration and you can register the snowflake account for free.
And then we are going to use the preset service which is a managed super set by tool.
So one of the more popular BI tools out there to connect to snowflake and create a simple executive
dashboard.
Now as you start as an analytics engineer in a company, there are several requirements you need to
take care of.
You want to work in a system where modeling changes are easy to follow and easy to revert.
So if you can make your model code, it helps a lot because you can version control it right?
And you can track all the changes and collaborate and revert.
You also want to make sure that as you have many steps in your data pipeline, for example, many views
and tables which feed each other and depend on each other, you want to make sure that these dependencies
are explicit.
So the framework knows in which order to execute different steps in your pipeline to get to the transformed
result.
A good data set that's ready for analytics.
Also, you want to make sure that it's not only explicit, but it's also easy to overview.
So these dependencies are easy to explore easy to overview.
And this comes with a bunch of feature requirements from a tool when you build your pipeline.
So for example a tool should be able to expose this on a UI.
And let's see what else.
You also need to make sure that when your pipeline is working, then you can test for data quality.
You can make sure that your pipeline works as expected, and if there are any errors, then you want
to get alerted somehow, right?
So these are probably the basic requirements.
And as you will see, as we progress in building out our business logic, we will have also a few extra
requirements.
For example, we will have tables that are incremental tables.
So event based tables new events might come in.
And we don't want to rebuild the whole table.
We only want to add records, add new records at the end of the table.
And also in some cases we want to manage slowly changing dimensions.
That is managing and keeping the history of any Change so we can go back in time.
So these are some of the main requirements of a data analytics tool.
And DBT is great for managing these requirements.
So let's continue with our next step.
In the beginning I will ask you to follow me and uh, do a few steps of well, first of all, snowflake
registration.
So we will use snowflake, the data warehouse solution for this course.
Then we are going to import our data into snowflake.
Then we will show you how you can install DBT on windows and on the Mac.
Those come in separate videos.
So feel free to just pick whichever you prefer.
And once all of this is set up, we will be ready to go.
So let's get started.
```

## Seeds and Sources {#seeds-and-sources}

### Snowflake's Authentication types and the Key-Pair Authentication {#snowflakes-authentication-types-and-the-key-pair-a}

```
Let me quickly explain to you what we're going to do when we are setting up our tables and users and
everything in snowflake.
Now, as of November 2025, snowflake made quite some changes in how you can authenticate into it.
So one is a username and password based authentication.
You can have a multifactor app on your device that works well, but it doesn't work for so-called service
account.
A service account is a non-human entity accessing snowflake, for example.
Our project.
It's not a human, it's a piece of software.
So that is a service account, which means that we won't be able to use a single username and password
authentication for that.
The second one is that you can generate a personal access token in snowflake.
So a random string basically and authenticate with that.
But in order to use that you will need to set up network policies in snowflake.
So that's more like an enterprise grade setting.
Then there is single sign on.
Single sign on means that you integrate snowflake with your Google Workspace or Office 365 suite, or,
you know, whatever, and you go through these providers.
That's something we don't want to do because we don't want to overcomplicate things.
And the fourth option, and as you can see, this is what we're going to use is the key pair based authentication.
Now I want to show you how the key pair based authentication works.
So you have a basic idea about what we are doing.
Disclaimer I love this topic.
I could speak about this for hours.
This is what fuels digital signatures and digital certificates and Https and everything around it.
So if you are an analytics engineer or a data engineer, I would suggest you to go and look up the public
key authentication or PKI infrastructure to understand how this whole encryption and communication works.
It's super useful, but here comes a one minute intro.
You, as the user will create and generate with a piece of software, a so-called key pair.
A key pair consists of a private key that you never give anyone, and the public key, which is public.
So you can just give it to someone if they require it.
Now these two keys are mathematically interconnected, which means that if you encrypt the message with
the public key, then it will be only the private key holder who can decrypt this message.
That's the main thing.
So what we're gonna do is we're going to generate our key pair for us.
We will register our public key into snowflake, keep our private key with us.
And then when we want to authenticate, snowflake will be able to come up with a message, encrypt it
with the public key we provided, send the encrypted message to us and then we will be able to decode
it, decrypt it, thus proving snowflake that we are the actual key holders.
Now, in reality, the whole thing is somewhat more sophisticated than that, but you get the intuition.
So we're going to use our private key in our DBT project, and also later in our BI tool.
And with the help of these two keys we will be able to authenticate and connect to snowflake.
The good news is that you don't need to worry about any of this, because I created an automatic setup
script for the whole thing that you will find in the course resources.
But also here is the link for it, which will generate the key pair for you, and then set up the database
tables or Airbnb listings and reviews.
Setup a snowflake user and its permissions and also set up a so-called preset user, which will be our
user and its permissions.
I would encourage you to follow me in the next video and go through this automated setup in a few minutes,
but if you are very, very much interested in the details I'm setting up after the next video.
A longer video where I explain every little step that this automatic setup script does for you.
So if you want to understand it, feel free to go through that too.
But even if you choose the manual path, I would suggest you set up everything with the automatic application
and then just follow along the video to see how I did everything manually.
Let's go on with the setup.
```

### dbt Project Setup {#dbt-project-setup}

```
Finally, we are ready to set up our dbt project.
Let's do this.
Ensure that your virtual lamp is activated and we will go and say dbt init.
And one important piece, I'm going to get back to this in a second, is
dash dash skip profile setup.
And then our project name and our project name is Airbnb.
So please execute this one.
Of course, you will find this command in the course resources markdown.
Okay.
It's up and running.
Let's open this folder in your editor, which in my case is Visual Studio Code.
You can see that we have a bunch of subfolders and files in this folder, which of course we
will explore in great detail later in the course.
For now, let's just take the profile.com.
I will go and simply drag and drop it in there.
But you can just copy and paste it in there in any way you like.
The main thing is that you want to end up with a profile.com, which has all the connection details to Snowflake.
Let's see if it works.
First of all, as you open the editor, you might want to re-enable your virtual environment.
As you can see, mine has been already re-enabled.
And now you can say dbt debug.
So execute dbt debug.
dbt debug will take a look at your project and tell you if the whole file structure and everything is consistent and if it can connect to your data warehouse.
It should only take a second or two to execute.
And it says that our profile is okay.
Our dbt project YAML is okay.
Git is found.
If you don't have Git installed, you might see an error or Git not found here.
Don't worry about that for now.
You don't need Git for the core part of this course.
Then it tells you all about the connections and it tries to connect to Snowflake.
If you see connection okay, you're golden.
If you have problems connecting to Snowflake when you execute dbt debug, the most common issues are that you are either behind the VPN or that you are behind a corporate proxy or a corporate firewall.
In this case, I would like to ask you to switch off the VPN and ensure that you use a laptop and the network that provides you with unconstrained network access.
So you might want to use your personal laptop and you run into issues or just switch wifi and use a wifi network that doesn't constrain your network access.
Let's talk a bit about why we use the skip profile setup switch.
We did this because by default dbt tries to set up your profile file, not in your project folder, but in your home folder.
For earlier dbt versions, the default location for your profiles folder was a folder called .dbt inside your home folder.
This has changed with recent dbt versions and now your default profiles folder is your project folder.
But I want to discuss a little bit about it.
So by default, you would go to your home folders .dbt folder, both in windows and on a Mac.
And here you would find a profile.
Now you won't find it here and that's okay.
I just set this up for you for demo.
And the reason behind this was that generally speaking, you don't want to pack your connection details, which has your private key and.
And other sensitive information in your project folder, because you don't want to submit it to get.
And of course your connection might be different depending on if you are on your own laptop or if you are on a production server.
So if you work with production dbt apps, I would suggest you to either copy the profiles.yaml to your project folder automatically through a CI CD pipeline.
And exclude it from Git.
So never submit it or just use it in an arbitrary location.
Regardless where you have your profiles.yaml, you can always execute dbt with the dead-profile.dr parameter, which specifies the path to your profiles.yaml.
In this course for simplicity, we use the profiles.yaml default location, which is our project folder.
So let's take a look at the profiles.yaml file.
The profiles.yaml has a profile name, which we will reference later in our project file.
So we know which profile to use.
And then dbt can have multiple targets.
For example, you can have a target.
So a connection to your dev environment.
And we would have like a QA for testing and a prod environment if you wish.
Here in this example, we only have a dev environment for now.
So we have a single target, which is called dev.
And also we are setting this as a default target.
And everything that comes in between is your connection detail.
We use a snowflake type connection.
This is our snowflake account.
We are authenticating with the dbt user that we set up, which has the role transform.
It's again, a role that we set up with the automatic setup script.
Here is a private key that the setup script generated for us.
And the private key also has a passphrase.
It is optional to have a passphrase, but our BI tool will require a private key, which is protected by a passphrase.
So I just added a single digit Q as a passphrase when we generated our private key.
The database we want to use is Airbnb.
This is because our automated snowflake setup set up an Airbnb database for us with a schema called row, which has all the host listing and reviews tables.
Okay.
And the output schemas will be dev.
So this is where we want to then later generate our tables.
For the sake of simplicity, we also instruct dbt to only work on one model and the other.
So we don't get into parallel execution problems.
And the snowflake, there is a default data warehouse that we will use.
And it is called compute WH.
This is how our profile.chaml has been set up.
And now you're up and running with dbt.
There is one last, but very important thing to avoid any confusion.
I'm going to use sometimes Visual Studio course terminal and sometimes a standalone terminal application.
It doesn't matter.
Right?
I would suggest you just use Visual Studio course terminal because that gives you, even if you are on Windows, the closest experience to my environment here on a Mac.
And another super important detail is that you will see sometimes Airbnb here as my project folder name, but sometimes you will see dbt.learn.
The reason for that is that recently dbt.labs has trademarked the dbt.learn term.
So now in the new videos, I go with the Airbnb project name, but in some earlier videos, you will see that the folder I work on is dbt.learn.
It doesn't matter.
You will always see Airbnb and everything will work 100% the same.
So let's move on and get our hands dirty with dbt.

## Debugging with Logging {#debugging-with-logging}

### Models Overview {#models-overview}

```
Modules are the basic building blocks of your business logic, and also the basic building blocks of
a DBT project.
You can think about modules as SQL definitions, which then materialize as tables or views, but we
will see that there are much more to them.
Probably for now, what you need to know about modelers is that they are stored as SQL files in the
models folder, and they are not only simple SQL select statements, but they come with some extra features.
For example, models can reference other models.
So DBT knows some semantic dependencies between those.
And also you can use different scripts and macros in your models.
Now let's see how they work in action.
```


## Using Variables {#using-variables}

### Theory: CTE - Common Table Expressions {#theory-cte-common-table-expressions}

```
Common table expressions are very easy and simple.
CTS essentially help us write readable and maintainable code quickly.
By definition, they are a temporary named result set.
In reality, CTS are great because the result remains in memory during the execution of a select, insert,
update, delete or merge statement.
The syntax of a CT would look something like this.
The With clause is basically telling the database that we are going to create a common table expression,
after which you can give a name to our CT or more precisely to our result set that we can reference
later.
The column names are completely optional, and they are used for setting up aliases for the column names
that will come out of the common table expression.
The CTE query is a Select statement, and in reality this is just a temporary table that will be referenced
from the from clause like other everyday tables.
The referenced CT part is where we will execute and reference our common table expression.
Let's take a look at an example.
Let's say we extracted raw data from the source systems via Airbnb.
The data is 100% uncleansed and we will want to do transformations on it.
In step one, we create the common table expression that we named raw listings.
Then in step two, we have an inner query where we selected the source listings as they are all the
columns that we could get our hands on basically.
Then in step three, which is an outer query, we query the CT name that we have provided, which is
raw listings.
We selected not all columns of the source, but only specific columns on which we performed the simplest
of all changes a rename of the ID column to listing ID to make it more obvious.
That's it.
Now keep in mind that we won't dive into more advanced CTS as they are outside the scope of this course.
However, some general takeaways I would like to share.
So although a CT has very similar functionality to a view, CTS will not store the definition in metadata.
We prefer CTS because they are extremely readable, they are easy to maintain and they make complex
queries more understandable.
Furthermore, a given query can be divided into separate simple logical building blocks, which can
be then used to build more complex CTS until the final result set gets generated.
Lastly, CTS can be also defined in functions, stored procedures, triggers, or even views.
We're going to use CTS many times throughout the course, so you will see a ton of examples soon.
See you in the next video.
```

## Capstone Project {#capstone-project}

### Materializations Overview {#materializations-overview}

```
Materializations are different ways how your models can be stored and managed in the data warehouse.
There are four built in Materializations view table.
Incremental and ephemeral view is the default and this is a materialization you already saw in our previous
lectures.
When you pick the materialization view, then your model will be represented as a view.
It's so simple.
There are other materializations like the table.
If you pick the table materialization, your model will be materialized as an actual table.
And every time you re-execute your DBT flow, this table will be recreated.
Then there is an incremental materialization which is still a table, but a materialization type created
for incremental loads like event data or any fact data.
Now in our case in our Airbnb use case this will be reviewed.
So as reviews flow in we will increment our tables with new reviews.
And then there is the ephemeral materialization, which is very much a virtual materialization.
It is a no materialization materialization.
It means that if you tell a model to be an ephemeral materialization, it will be converted into a CTE
on in any downstream model.
But it won't be in the data warehouse per se.
So when do you want to use this method?
I believe if you have experience with data warehousing, it's very much common sense.
But let me just sum it up real quick.
So as you know, a view materialization a view is a very lightweight thing in a data warehouse.
It's more like a select which acts as if it was a table kind of right.
So you want to use it if you want to have a lightweight representation, when you don't want to recreate
a table at every execution, but you don't want to use it if you read from the same view many times,
repeatedly in the same pipeline.
Because remember, a view is just a select.
Every time you access a view, the underlying select needs to be executed.
If you want to have a efficient way to work with with the militarization that can be accessed repeatedly,
you probably want to go with the table militarization.
Now, what are the cons of the table militarization?
It's that it needs to be recreated every time you execute your pipeline.
So it takes some extra time.
But when it's there, your transform data is there.
So it will be quite fast to read from that data.
But in many cases you have data which is incrementing.
So event level data, right.
For example, orders in a in a store solution or in our case reviews.
In this case you don't want to have your table to be recreated every time.
You only want to ingest the new data and append to the end of the table.
So incremental authorizations will help you there.
And as you will see, DBT manages much of the heavy lifting for you here.
And then it can happen that you simply define an intermediate step between models.
And you just don't want to publish and advertise it in the data warehouse.
And in this case, ephemeral metaphysicians can work very well.
So we will see an example to all of those in the upcoming lessons.
So let's get started.
```

## REFERENCE - Python Installation {#reference-python-installation}

### Seeds and Sources Overview {#seeds-and-sources-overview}

```
When you do modeling, your input data can come from two sources.
Either it's already in the data warehouse because it has been integrated by an integration tool like
Fivetran or Airbyte, or any custom ETL tool or ETL process, or it's a smaller data set that you want
to send from your laptop directly to the data warehouse.
And the latter is called a seed.
And if you have the data already in the data warehouse, that will be called a source in DBT.
And DBT provides tools for defining seeds and sources, and also to make sure that your sources are
up to date and get alerting on stale, outdated sources.
So let's see how this works in practice.
```

### Source Freshness Checks {#source-freshness-checks}

```
In a production setting, you might be able to create the most perfect pipeline.
But there is one thing you don't have control over and these are your sources.
The sources are in production many times, managed by other teams or third parties, so you don't really
have control on like in what format they come in.
If the format changes or if the source is fresh or stale.
So for example, your third party or your the other team, it just stops updating the source.
We will take a look later at how you can test sources of course, but here I want to show you a feature
that helps you check for source freshness.
We are gonna create a simple configuration property to the source, and with the help of this, we will
be able to check if the data in the source is fresh enough.
Now, how do you know if the data in a source is fresh?
What you can do is that you define a column.
We will call it a loaded at field.
And DBT will take a look at this column.
In our case this will be the date column.
And simply check how old the most recent date record in that column is.
It's always a good practice to have a date column, either for fact data or also for dimension data
to have an Updatedat or Createdat column.
So you can see when your data gets there.
If you remember in earlier videos, we added an extra row to row reviews, but based on how you're pacing
out this course, that might be maybe just a few minutes in the past, but maybe a few days or even
weeks in the past.
Right now, we're gonna implement a source freshness check and find it out.
This requires a few lines of code, so let me just paste this here for you.
So what you want to do is that for the reviews column you create a new config.
And under the config you specify the loaded field, which in our case remember is date.
Write date as the name of the column.
And then for the freshness, we can decide how strict we want to be with the source freshness.
So we can say that, for example, if the most recent record in our source table is older than one hour
in this case, then we want to have a warning.
But if it's older than 24 hours, then we want to have an error.
Let's save this file and see it in action.
I can say source freshness and by executing this, let's see how old my most recent record is in the
table.
Oh, no.
I received an error.
So it's, uh, very old.
When you take this to production, you should know that if there is an error, then the exit code of
this command will be non-zero, right?
So if I say give me the exit code, it's one.
Which means that if this was integrated into a CI CD pipeline, then the pipeline would fail.
Now this specific command is Linux and Mac specific, but I'm giving you the windows command to in course
resources.
So let me just comment this out so I can demo a warning for you.
Okay.
Let's execute this.
Obviously our source will be stale.
It will be older than one hour in the past.
So now it gives me a warning that you can pick up from the logs if you want.
But if I ask if this source freshness failed, then you will see a zero exit code, which means that
from the, for example, a ci CD point of view, that's okay.
You don't need to fail the whole pipeline.
All in all, you want to ensure that any time you take it to production, you want to apply source freshness
on third party sources.
```

### Learning Objectives - Snapshots {#learning-objectives-snapshots}

```
In this section, you're going to see how DBT helps you keep history as your data changes.
This is done with the help of type two.
Slowly changing dimensions.
A topic we already covered in our theoretical lessons.
You will see and understand how DBT handles these type two slowly changing dimensions.
And also you will understand what are the different snapshotting strategies DBT supports.
And we are going to go on and implement snapshots both for our existing table and our host table.
So let's get started.
```

### Snapshots Overview {#snapshots-overview}

```
Let's take a look at how snapshots are implemented in DBT.
First, a quick recap of how slowly changing dimensions work.
So this will be a demo of type two slowly changing dimensions.
Let's assume you have a dimension table.
Let's assume that's our host table.
And we have Alice and Bob in here, and Bob has his email address set to Bob.
Dot Airbnb at gmail.com.
But now Bob wants to update his email address, and the new email address will be Bob's new address
at gmail.com.
In a analytical data pipeline, somehow you don't only want to overwrite this data, but you want to
make sure that you keep history of this change.
And the way DBT handles this, and this is a very much a best practice in the data warehousing world.
It's called type two slowly changing dimensions.
So what it does is that it takes the original table, the original columns, and it adds two extra columns
which simply define from which point in time is this record running from, and which point in time is
it very true?
If it's still a current record, you always have a null at value two, and if there is a change, then
the value two record will be set to the actual date of the change, and the new record will be emitted
with the new value, with the new starting point and null as DVD value two.
So you can always see the current version by filtering to date.
Value two equals to null, and you can still see the whole history, because at every change there is
a new record appended to the table.
Now you have two strategies to get a slowly changing dimension rolling.
One is the so-called timestamp strategy.
If you can define a unique key on your source table and you have an updated at field which restores
what was the last time a certain field was updated then based on this, DB2 will be able to record any
change.
That's simple right?
But even if you don't have these, you have another strategy which is called check and recheck.
You can simply specify one or more columns or even all the columns in your source data, and DB2 will
take a look if any of these columns changed and if any of these columns change, then DB2 will add new
records.
So you can kind of define a unique key like that.
Okay.
So let's see how this works in practice.
```

### Create a Snapshot {#create-a-snapshot}

```
Let's go and get our feet wet with snapshots.
Now, as we all know, in a real world setting, the source data might change.
So for example, if you take the listings, then the hosts will update different properties of this
table.
For example, the minimum nights someone has to stay to rent this Airbnb.
What you don't want in a real world pipeline is to lose track of this information, which means that
if someone updates a certain property, then what you don't want is just to push through the whole data
to the pipeline and lose the history of this data.
So now what we are going to do is to create this snapshot logic so we can track every change.
Starting from DBT 1.9, snapshot implementation is managed by YAML files.
And you can put the YAML files for your snapshot definitions either to the snapshot path, which in
our case and by default is snapshots.
Or you can just put them anywhere inside the models folder.
I'm going to create our listing snapshot file and then execute a few SQL commands.
And of course you will find all of these commands and the code on GitHub in the course resources.
So let's just create a new file and let's call it row listings snapshot dot YAML.
You start with snapshots.
And under snapshots you will be able to define one or more snapshot definitions.
We will go with one snapshot per file in this course.
But in a production setting it's very much up to you how you want to organize your snapshots.
I would suggest you, but this is just a subjective thing.
I would suggest you to just put your snapshot files besides your model definitions with the underscore
snapshot prefix.
So if someone takes a look at your model definition it.
They will see in the file directly that oh there is a snapshot for this file.
So maybe I want to take a look at the snapshot logic two here just to adhere to the.
Convention.
So to say I will put everything into snapshot folders and I will go with.
A single snapshot per file snapshot as a name.
And in our case what I want to give it as a name is.
Skid row listings.
Then we can specify the relation which is the source.
Right.
So we want to.

And then let's configure the snapshot.
Let's take a look at the contents of this model first.
Our primary key is the ID column.
And then what I want you to remember is that we have this column called Minimum Nights.
And then as you see we also have an updated ad column.
Now this Updatedat column contains information about then this specific record was updated for the last
time.
And this is exactly what our snapshot logic will take a look at.
So if you can ensure that you keep the Updatedat column updated, then the snapshot engine will be able
to spot every change.
So it will keep a state of the latest update values.
And every time you re-execute the snapshot logic, it will be able to compare the recent updated values
to the ones it stored.
And if it sees a change, then it knows that we have now a new state for a certain record, so it will
end the life cycle of the old record and create a new record with the new values.
Let's go on and pass this info to DBT.
I will say that the unique key is ID right the ID column.
The updated add field is updatedat.
And you also want to tell DBT this strategy to use for Snapshotting.
We are going to use the timestamp strategy, but there are basically three strategies for snapshotting.
One is timestamp.
When DBT takes a look at the Updatedat column and the changes in the Updatedat column.
This is what we are going to implement, and I believe that also in a production setting this is like
the cleanest solution you can go.
It's very, very explicit.
But then there are also two other strategies.
One is the check strategy.
With the check strategy you would tell DBT which columns it should closely monitor.
And if DBT then sees that there is a change in the values of those columns.
Then it will create a new record for every affected record.
And the third strategy, if you are a super, super advanced user and you want to implement your own
checks, then you will be able to create a macro and with the help of this macro, just implement how
the checks should be executed.
But again in production you will probably need the timestamp strategy.
So this is what we are implementing here.
Okay.

How can the snapshot engine pick up this change?
There is a configuration variable for that of course, and it is called hard delete.
So what should happen if someone deletes a record from the original table?

### Learning objectives - Tests {#learning-objectives-tests}

```
As soon as you take your DVD pipeline from your dev computer into a production system, you will very
quickly understand that testing is probably the most essential component of DBT.
By the end of this section, you will understand what unit tests are and data tests in DBT.
How these tests can be defined.
What are generic data tests?
What are singular data tests and how you can implement these in practice?
Let's get started.
```

### Tests Overview {#tests-overview}

```
DVT works with two types of tests unit tests and data tests.
It also supports a search feature that I consider part of testing called contracts.
The unit tests test transformations with a small sample of hard coded data you provide.
You can think of these tests as unit tests in a software engineering project.
Then there are data tests which check the integrity and quality of the data in the data warehouse.
So not from a mock hard coded data set, but the actual data.
You can think of these as integration tests in software engineering.
And then we have contracts that enforce the schema of a model.
So you can catch if a model schema changes accidentally.
Data tests are probably the most sophisticated of the three.
DBT has two types of datasets singular and generic.
Generic tests are common tests that are available plug and play in DBT, and there are four types of
built in generic tests.
First, you can ensure that the column only has unique values.
Then you can also ensure that the column has no null values.
You can also define a set of accepted values.
A column must then comprise only of the values in this list, and you can also define relationships,
which is a little bit like foreign keys in a database.
Now if that wouldn't be enough for a production use case, DBT provides you with the possibility to
write singular tests, which you can then implement as SQL queries.
The evaluation logic is very simple.
When DBT executes the query that you wrote and they return any record, then your test will fail.
If you see this in practice in the next videos, we'll also see later in the course that you can define
your own custom generic tests too, so you can extend generic tests with your own implementation, and
you'll see that you can also import tests from third party DBT packages.
Now let's go and get our hands dirty.
```

### Unit Tests {#unit-tests}

```
If you want to test a core functionality of a single step in a pipeline.
So a model, then unit tests are the best tools to use.
They are really like unit tests in software engineering.
They don't work on the actual data warehouse, but you specify some input data and an expected output
and they will go on, take the input data, apply the model on it and check the results against the
expected output.
Let's see this in action.
We are going to test the March Full Moon reviews, which if you remember, is this table that joins
the factory views with the seed full moon dates and creates a table that serves as a basis to check
whether Full Moon affects reviews.

Of course you can put it under test.
You can just create any subfolder and put your unit test YAML file in there.
Or you can use modules if you want.
In this case, because a unit test doesn't have anything to do with the actual data warehouse, and
it only checks the core functionality of the model, I will just put it into a file.
Besides our model definition.
So they hang together in models slash mart, I'm going to create a new file which I will call unit test
YAML.
But this is an arbitrary name.
It can be anywhere called anything YAML.

You can give a unit test name.
So I will just say unit test.

### Data Contracts {#data-contracts}

```
Another feature that you might find very useful in production is called contracts.
I put it into the test section because I think it very much associates with the actual testing of your
data set.
But basically what data contracts do is that you can hard code the schema of a model in a YAML file.
And this way you can catch very early if someone changes the schema of your model accidentally.
Let's open our schema YAML and implement a contract for the DreamHost glance model.
If you take a look at this model, you will see that it has a few columns.
Host ID host name is super host Createdat Updatedat.
The host ID is an integer, hostname is a string is super host is actually a string.
It's true and false values, but it's represented as a string on the data warehouse level.
Createdat and Updatedat are obviously timestamps.
You can implement contracts in YAML file such as schema YAML by simply adding extra properties to the
model's attributes.
As a first step.
As a first step, let's list the columns for DreamHost clients.
I just keep on adding all the columns.
So we have host ID and the rest of the columns.
If you take a look, you will see that at this point this is just a standard model definition, such
as we did with dim listings cleansed.
But now we are adding the contract part and this will be called data type.
So we are specifying the data type that.
For example the data type for host id is an integer.
DB2 has its own keywords for host type, so it can stay data warehouse agnostic.
But if you check out the contracts documentation, you will see that you can be much more specific to
your data warehouse when defining a data type.
Hostname is a string.
A super host, as we discussed, is a string, Updatedat and createdat are timestamp, and there is
a last piece you want to add is that you define that this is an enforce contract.
So if the model schema doesn't adhere to the contract, your DB2 run should fail.
So you can simply add the config where you can say that you want to have a contract which is enforced.
Let's execute DBT run and it should work, right?
Because we set up this contract based on the actual schema of dim listings cleansed.
And as you can see, it's working amazing.
Now let's assume that someone wants to make a change to dim host cleansed.
And for example, one change that a teammate let's say would do is that they wanted to improve this
model by replacing the host, which is a string to an actual Boolean value.
And maybe the easiest way to go in snowflake would be to use Snowflake's inline if functionality.
So if the super is super host is true, then let's return true.
Otherwise it falls under the same name.
So let's just save this file and let's try to execute DBT run again.
And it's quoted.
Now you can see right away that it says that the dim listings host cleansed.
Materialization failed.
And here you get the actual explanation.
It says that the host column by I contract should be taxed.
But here the definition is boolean which is a data type mismatch.
So this way you will be able to catch data type changes and name changes.
And also if you take a look at the documentation you will find that depending on your data warehouse,
you can also catch changes for different constraints.
Like if a not nullability of a column has changed or not.
So what would happen in this case?
Probably your teammates would realize that this is a contracted model, so it is important to stay in
that specific schema.
And if they want to update it, they should be more careful.
And maybe take a look at what effects this update would have.
Oh, and don't forget to revert your DreamHost cleansed.
So you end up with a working DBT pipeline.
Okay, so this should be simply a superhost.
Now maybe a last remark.
Contracts are quite a new feature in DBT, so in upcoming videos when we, for example, work with documentations,
you might not see this DreamHost cleans contract in the schema dot YAML file.
Don't worry about that.
Just leave this here and everything will work fine.

### Setting the Tests'  Severity: Warning vs Error {#setting-the-tests-severity-warning-vs-error}

```
Sometimes you want us not to fill the whole pipeline, but to trigger a warning.
Let me show you how you can achieve this with custom generic tests.
So let's set up a crazy large number as our minimum row count here, and execute our test on the listings
class.
And this must fail, right?
Let's just wait a second to see if it fails.
As expected, this has failed.
And here is our failing test.
Okay, but we don't want the whole pipeline to fail.
But we just want to give a warning.
That's again, super simple.
You just add the new attribute to the minimum row count test and you say severity is one.
So we only want to have warnings for this test.
Let's just re-execute this.
This is now completed with a single warning.
And here is the test with the warning.
So that's, uh, amazing.
Now, one last thing I want to show you about this is that if you want to generalize this warning,
if you let's say you want to say that actually minimum row count should by default Fault.
Give a warning.
Then you can just remove this one from here and go directly to the test implementation.
And then just come here and add a config directly to the test.
So you can say I want to add the config.
And by default the severity should be a warning.
So if I re-execute this, then even though I don't have anything set up here.
So based on the YAML this should be an error.
But I'm telling it in the test definition that this should be a warning, and you see it right there
that it's actually a warning.
And of course what you can do that if you have a test that warns by default, but you want to be very
strict and you want to say that actually for this specific case, I want to have an error as a severity,
then this works too.
So you can override the default behavior in the YAML.
You see now it's failing.
So we are all good.
So let's just put this back to 1000 to ensure that you have a working pipeline as we're moving on with
the course.
```

### Jinja Basics {#jinja-basics}

```
Let's step up our DBT toolkit so we can extend our projects even more.
We will do this with the help of two components.
One is called ginger and the other one is called macros.
With the help of ginger, you will be able to do actual programming in your DB2 sequence, and with
the help of macros, you will be able to create reusable components that you can use as tests or custom
blocks in your DB2 sequence.
Let's start with Ginger.
Now, you've already seen a bunch of ginger tags in your DB2 projects.
For example.
The things between the small curly braces.
But what I want you to understand here is basically the internal structure of ginger and the basic building
blocks.
So you have a good mental model about what and how you can achieve by using it.
Ginger is a templating language, and you can think about it as a tool to do programming inside your
sequence, we will go through variables, conditionals, loops, functions, built ins, and macros.
Let's build from the ground up.
Ginger has three types of basic building blocks.
The simplest are.
Comments.
Comments are well.
Comments.
Everything you put between comment text will be actually deleted and ignored.
When the ginger template is rendered, then we get statements which can be variable assignments, as
you can see here.
Or if conditionals or loops or macro calls and so on.
And then we have expressions which is something you're already very familiar with.
For example we put the href tags inside so-called ginger expressions.
You will learn much more about variable handling in later sections of the courses.
But now I just want to give you a quick, hands off example of what you can achieve with these simple
building blocks.
```

Source.

or anywhere in macros.
It doesn't need to be a pure SQL.
What you put between these if else tags.

because this is loop last from the dim listings cleansed model.

of the jinja text.
Right.
So these will be compiled into jinja.
But whatever you put in here, let them be space steps enters.
Those will be there.

### Learning Objectives - Documentation {#learning-objectives-documentation}

```
Now that you have a good understanding of how to work with DBT models, how to configure them and implement
them, and also how to test them and create your own macros and packages.
We arrive to our last crucial point in the base DBT feature set, which is documentation.
You will see in this section how you can use DBT for building your documentation and document your models.
Serve your documentation as HTML pages.
Also, some special use cases like how to work with the overview page and assets like images, and we
will briefly discuss how to productionize documentation.
Let's get started.
```

### Documentation Overview {#documentation-overview}

```
The general idea of documentation in DBT is to keep documentation as close to the actual source code
to your analytics code as possible.
And this is always a best practice when you build a data infrastructure, because, you know, if you
disconnect documentation and you put it, let's say, to a completely standalone documentation service,
the documentation and your actual analytics code can diverge.
So because of this, DBT integrates that very, very well to avoid this situation.
You can work with documentations two ways.
Either you can put simple documentation pieces into your YAML files like the schema YAML.
So the place where we defined our model, we will see how we can add descriptions there for documenting
models or tables or views or columns and so on.
And in some cases, if that's not enough, you can also write your own more comprehensive documentation
for any of the tables or columns in standalone markdown files.
DB2 will then compile this documentation into an HTML service, which you can then use either as a static
HTML set of assets.
And you can just, you know, put it somewhere and use it behind your own web server.
Or you can use the built in web server for development purposes, at least for serving your documentation
and just iterating on your documentation.
There are also two special cases here.
One is will be the project overview page.
You will see that later.
This has a special place which is called the overview MD.
You can write documentation for the overview page there in a standalone markdown file.
And you can also configure DBT to store different assets like Downloadables and images in a special
folder.
And then you can reference them from markdown files.
We will go through all of these use cases here.
Hands on in our next few lessons.
```

### Writing and Exploring Basic Documentation {#writing-and-exploring-basic-documentation}

```
First, we are going to take a look at how to create simple documentation and then generate HTML documentation
based on what we defined, and then how to start the documentation survey interface.
Simple documentation can be added to the YAML files to your model description.
And you can do this with models like adding documentation to schema or adding documentation to sources.
But you can also add documentation to macros and tests.
Let's document our model.
Please open schema YAML and then add documentation to dim listings list.

In DBT you simply add a new property.
Here you call it description and then you add your documentation definition.
So for example the dim listings cleans tables description will be that this is a clean table which contains
Airbnb listings.
Then you can go and do the same for columns.
So for each column I can add the description tag.
This would be for example the primary key for the listing.
And now my listing ID column is documented.
Let's do the same with host ID.
I'm going to copy paste this and you can get it from the class resources.
So host ID is the host ID.
And then we also have room type.
Let's just add a description to this too.

### Analyses {#analyses}

```
Many times all you need is to execute an ad hoc query.
You don't want to create a model on the top of it, or you don't want to have it materialize anyway.
You simply want to execute a query, but still use macros and templating system and model.
Reference system and analysis are here to help you with this analysis.

created a model.

a full moon or not.

sentiment.

here.
And if you want to execute them against your data warehouse, you can execute the DBT compile with DBT
compile.
We go through all of your sqls and compile it to actually SQL.
And then the queries will be here in target and then compiled and AB compiled DBT learn and analysis.
And here we go.
This is SQL.
And as you see now the template has been parsed.

### Hooks {#hooks}

```
Let's talk about hooks.
Hooks is an exciting feature that lets you customize what SQL DB executes.
What does that mean?
This means that you can inject SQL or macros that are then executed when DB stores dividends, or when
it starts to create a model, or when it created a model, and so on.
You can configure hooks on the project subfolder or even at the model level.
And there are several types that you can work with.
One that I will show you an example of is the Oran startup.
The Oran Star is executed when you execute DBT run or DBT build or DBT test and so on.
So at the beginning of a DB run.
But there are hooks like on run end, which executes as the very last step of your DB execution, and
also for model seats and snapshots.
You can define pre hooks and post hooks.
So SQL or macros that are being executed before or after a certain model is created, or a seed or a
snapshot materialized.
Let's see this in action.
Please open your DBT project YAML.
And I want to give you an example of a very common business problem.
And that is auditing DBT run.
So for example when a certain model was materialized for the last time.
Now as a side note and a disclaimer, if you get serious about auditing, then you can find a bunch
of packages on DBT hub third party packages.
That helps you a lot with auditing.
But here I want to show you and demonstrate you the use of hooks to show you how easy it is to set up,
for example, an auditing behavior.
And what we want to do here is to create a table called audit Log.
And in this table, whenever a model is materialized, we want to put in the model name and the current
timestamp.
We are going to do this with using two types of hook.
With the Onstart hook we will ensure that our audit table exists, and then after each model creation
with the post hook, we are gonna insert a new record into this table.
So let's see how this works.
So first of all, let's create an Onstart hook which is a top level attribute in your YAML.
It is a longer piece of code.
So let me paste this here for you.
So on run start.
This is the hook name.
And what I want to do is I'm saying just create a table if it doesn't exist an audit log and create
this in the target schema.
You can use ginger expressions in the hooks.
So this will be dev right.
It will render as dev.
And we want to create a simple table which has the mother's name and a run timestamp when this model
was executed.
Now let's add the hook that will populate this table.
I want to add the global post hook.
So I'll put this here under Airbnb.
So it gets executed after each and every model creation.
So I say I want to create a post hook, which is a list because I can have multiple hooks for each hook
definition.
And all I want to do is I will say insert into this audit log table a few values.
And here I want to have the model name.
So I just say this.
It's a special jinja tag that references the model that got created.
And I want to pass the current timestamp.
Okay, this looks good.
Well my default schema is already dev, but just to be very precise, let me also add the target schema
here.
So I will just say insert into target schema dot audit log values.
So the model name and the current timestamp.
Let's go and execute the DBT run and the run.
You will see explicitly that we have a start hook that's getting executed and that it has been executed
successfully.
So that's great.
And as you can see, this has been completed now successfully.
Let me go to our snowflake UI and see whether we have a new table called Audit Log.
Let's go to Airbnb and Dev and tables.
And yes I have audit log here.
If I click definition you can see that it looks good right.
And I can just say like select star from.
Let me be very explicit airbnb.dev audit log.
And if I execute it here we go.
So we have all our models that's being materialized with a current timestamp in the database.
This is the hook functionality of DBT.
Again remember it's super useful for executing sqls either at the beginning or at the end, or before
or after each model creation.
Now, I would like to ask you to stop for a second and just think about what could be good use cases

### Grants - Managing Permissions in dbt the Modern Way {#grants-managing-permissions-in-dbt-the-modern-way}

```
Now that you are familiar with hooks, let me tell you about a very common technical problem that comes
up with data warehouses and DBT, and that is that in certain scenarios, there might be such a set
of permissions in the data warehouse that you are able to create a model.
So create a table or a view.
You might able to set the permissions for this view, but you cannot read newly accessed models.
So tables or views.
And this is quite common.
What can you do in such a case.
Well you probably already know what you can do that after creating a model.
After materializing a model, you would be able to just say here, you know, like Grant select on this
model, uh, blah blah blah.
So you could either use a post hook on these models to grant select to yourself or for example, if,
uh, there is another user or role like a BI tool, then you could grant select to that BI tool.
And hooks were like the old way of doing this.
But what you can do in this case with modern versions, is to create a grant.
And let me show you how you can do that.
It's super easy.
I can just say Grant.
The plus is super important, right?
Because otherwise it will think that this is like a model family or a folder plus grants.
Sorry.
And for the grants, I can go and simply say that I want to create a select grant on basically every
model.
And I want to grant select to my transform role transform, which is my DBT user, and also the reporter
role which will be my BI connectors user.
Okay, so so simple.
No hooks nothing.
And this will result in DBT executing a grant after each model creation.
Now let's execute DBT run but hold.
Hold it for a bit.
So executing DBT run will not show you these grant commands.
But here is a sneak peek into future videos.
You can execute DBT, run debug and we will cover debugging into quite some detail later.
When you execute DBT debug, then you will see every SQL that is executed against the data warehouse.
Okay, if I now look for Grant then you will see that I have here.
For example after March full moon reviews I have grant select on Airbnb.
To transform and I also set to be executed to reporter.
So now you've optimized the fixing of every permission with the simple grants.
Operator.
Congrats.
```

### dbt Fusion - Introduction {#dbt-fusion-introduction}

```
In 2025, DVD fusion, an entirely new generation DTW engine, was released by DTW labs.
In this section, I would like to explain why fusion is a big deal, how it works, and what's behind
the scenes.
Along with DTW fusion, DTS Official Visual Studio Code extension has also been released.
We'll get our hands dirty with it and ensure that you will get the most out of it.
DTW fusion is a new feature compatible DTW engine that has been completely rewritten from the ground
up in rust.
It has magnitudes faster parsing engine and understands your DTW code way better.
It released under a source available license, which we will cover later in detail.
Let's move on and ensure that you understand how fusions, parser and compiler works.
I will show you the DTW fusion features available today, explain the license differences to DTW core,
and then we will go hands on with it along with the official Visual Studio Code extension.
I'll see you there.
```

### Exploring the Technical Differences Between dbt Fusion and dbt Core {#exploring-the-technical-differences-between-dbt-fu}

```
Let's take a look at the main technical differences between DBT fusion and DBT core, especially at
those two features where DBT fusion stands out the most.
Parsing and SQL comprehension.
Let's start with parsing.
We haven't discussed too much about how the ginger text, like the href tags and anything in curly braces,
gets compiled into actual SQL.
But here is what you want to know about it.
First, there is an analysis of all of these tags, such as refs, sources, and configs whatsoever.
And then Ginger translates this into a Python code and executes the Python code.
If you take a look at the manifest.json file in your target folder, you will see all these attributes
and the whole output of the parser.
But going through these two steps might take a lot of time, especially when it comes to larger projects.
And that is why DBT Fusion's rust implementation can speed up things big time by executing the whole
static analysis and manifest generation process in a single step, fully implemented in rust.
And this will result in magnitude faster parsing times.
So that's one thing.
And the other is SQL comprehension.
Action.
When you have a SQL file, then there are a few layers of data warehouse processing to go through before
executing your code.
First, your data warehouse parses your code for syntax errors so it can catch typos in keywords and
have a general understanding of what the SQL is about.
And then it proceeds to the next layer.
And in this layer, the compiler resolves the different expressions and maps them to data types and
actual columns in the data warehouse.
Once everything is compiled, the query can be optimized and executed.
Now here you see a more detailed view of the same parsing, compiling, optimizing, and executing circle.
DBT core leaves most of this work to the data warehouse so it parses your files.
So not in the sense of SQL parsing, but resolving the ginger text and so on.
And then it sends the SQL commands to the data warehouse.
So if there is any problem the data warehouse will catch it.
DBT fusion, on the contrary, has a built in parser and compiler.
And this way it can catch the errors on the developer's computer without the need of sending the code
to the data warehouse.
And this can significantly speed up development.
```

### dbt Fusion Feature Matrix {#dbt-fusion-feature-matrix}

```
In this short video you will see what DBT core features are available in DBT fusion.
Now at this point, I would like to remind you to return to this site when viewing this video.
As DBT fusion develops quite rapidly and features are added constantly.
I'm adding a link to this site to the course resources.
Generally speaking, if you use a popular cloud data warehouse technology and your code doesn't rely
on logging output or has highly customized logic, you should be able to migrate without issues at the
time of recording.
BigQuery, Databricks, redshift, and Snowflake data warehouses are supported.
Some of the limitations are custom authorizations and tooling that expects DBT course exact log output.
You will see later in the hands on sessions that DBT Fusion's log output differs significantly from
DBT course, even though the DBT labs team is working on improving the logging output at the moment.
Some of the DBT platforms or DBT cloud workload features are not supported.
But all in all, if you want to see if you can migrate, please scroll through this list and ensure
that your project uses those features that are not listed here.
Once again, do come to this site and check out the current status when you watch this video.
```

### Licence Changes {#licence-changes}

```
Let's take a look at DBT Fusion's new license, releasing DBT fusion with an Elastic License v2.
Received mixed feedbacks from the community, so I went on and reviewed the license pages.
That's something that I'm also linking for you in the courseware, and I also conducted interviews with
DBT labs employees about this change.
Now here is the thing.
As you most likely know, DBT core has an Apache two license, which allows you to do almost anything
you want with DBT core.
We've seen that this license can backfire.
I mean, Apache two can backfire, like in the case of Elasticsearch and MongoDB, where cloud providers
started offering these technologies as a paid service.
So based on this exact rationale.
DBT fusion is released under an Elastic License v2.
This means that you can use it for free and it will also be source available, but you cannot provide
DBT fusion as a service.
This means that if you have a project that uses DBT fusion for your data pipeline, you can use it in
your company without any restrictions, just like the core.
However, if you decide to sell DBT fusion the technology, I mean you will need to compensate DBT labs
for it.
Also, some of the enterprise features of DBT fusion may be proprietary, just as there are proprietary
parts of the DBT cloud platform offering today.
So the bottom line for you, as someone who wants to build with DBT is that this license change will
not affect you, so it shouldn't hold you back if you wish to migrate from core to fusion.
```

### Upgrade your Projects to Fusion and dbt 1.10+ using dbt-autofix {#upgrade-your-projects-to-fusion-and-dbt-110-using-}

All right so that was quick.

Ensure that you are in the projects folder.

You will see that it can do basically three things.
List duplicates, deprecations and jobs.
We are focusing here on Deprecations, which is the main functionality of this utility.
It can also remove duplicate YAML keys and work with upgrading DBT cloud platform jobs.
Let's execute DBT auto fix Deprecations.

did for us.

The auto fix won't change anything for you, but it's harmless to execute.

### Great Expectations Overview {#great-expectations-overview}

```
So far you've seen in this course how to create your singular tasks and generic tasks, and also custom
tasks.
So this is probably the best way to go if you want to test your data.
But you know, sometimes you don't need to.
You don't need to reinvent the wheel because someone else has done it already.
And this is the case with testing.
Many times what happens is that companies implement tests that are already out there in a very mature
and well maintained form.
And there is one library that's especially great in data testing.
And this is called Great Expectations.
When I worked with companies earlier I've seen and also implemented many so-called data sanity checks.
It can easily happen that your data pipeline works syntactically well, but for example, something
changes in your input data.
That leads to data errors.
So for example, let's assume our Airbnb example.

### Debugging dbt tests and Working with regular expressions {#debugging-dbt-tests-and-working-with-regular-expre}

```
Let's take a look at how you can verify the input format of strings in our source data.
And also we'll take a look at how you can debug built in test.
So let's take a look at prices.
If you remember if I select some of the price values from the source, they start with a dollar sign.
And they also contain dots.
So when I say select price from Airbnb.
Dot row dot row listings.
You see that they start with a dollar and they have a few dots in there.
And if you take a look at the dim listings cleansed.
So dim listings cleansed model.
Then you also see that we make a Uh, cleansing step here to convert the price into a number.
Right.
So we want to make sure that the price comes in a proper format.
So let's go and write a test for it.
We can write a test using a concept and a technique called regex.
Regex is a shorthand notation for regular expressions.
Regular expressions is a quite complex topic.
With the help of regular expressions, you can define patterns on how you want a string to look.
So, for example, a classic regular expression like uh thingy would be listing all the markdown files
in a folder.
So you would say like ls star dot markdown star.md right.
So that's already close to regular expressions.
But regular expressions.
It's it is an extensive language to describe these kinds of patterns and strings.
It is beyond the scope of this course to discuss regular expressions in depth.
But if you're not familiar with regular expressions, make sure you check them out because they are
a very essential part of the data engineering work.
So let's go and define the regular expression that matches this price format, which is again a dollar
sign, and then a number or two numbers, and then a dollar sign, and then again a few numbers.
With DBT expectations.
We have a column for that.
It means it's called expect column values to match regex.
So this is what we want to use on the price column.
And if you take a look.
It takes a regular expression, string or regular expression definition, and this function will make
sure that every value in that column matches this regular expression.
So let me go and go to my text editor.
Open sources.
Let's create a definition for the price column.
So name is price.
Let's define the tests for the price column here in the test.
I want to use the column values match regex.
And again I want to pass regex.
And here I'm going to type in a regular expression and also explain it to you.
So this will be a basic regular expression When you use the tilde, it says the value must start with
a certain with a certain value.
And here in our case, the value must start with the dollar sign.
But the dollar sign is a special sign in the regular expression world.
It means that, uh, the string should must end with a certain value.
So I need to escape this dollar with backslash.
But as I am in YAML, I will need to use double backslashes.
This is a little bit strange, but it happens if you chain multiple technologies together.
So YAML picks up backslashes as special characters and regular expressions pick up dollars as special
characters.
So in order to provide a single backslash to the regular expression engine, I need to escape this single
backslash with another backslash.

### Setting Default Values {#setting-default-values}

```
When you deal with missing variable values, you have three ways to go.

use this variable.

This letter I will show you in the next video.

of usage.

Let's open DBT project YAML.

### Using Date Ranges to Make Incremental Models Production-Ready {#using-date-ranges-to-make-incremental-models-produ}

```
Please open the Factory views SQL if you can recall our implementation here for an incremental model
is the standard naive DB implementation.
That means that if it's a full refresh, then DBT won't set the incremental flag, which means that
DBT takes a look at the incremental table.
If it doesn't exist yet, then it says it's a full refresh.
It's incremental, will evaluate to false, and if your table is already there, then it's incremental
evaluates to true.
And in the standard implementation from the source table, we take all those values that are not in
the target table yet by adding a simple date based condition.
Now in production, usually this is not enough.
In production you want to many times backfill different date ranges because things go wrong and you
want to reload them and so on and so on.
And it's a standard practice to run incremental models with a parameterization of a date range to fill
in and execute.
I will show you a solution to that.
I'll come and copy paste this and you will find this in the course.
Resources.

Let's take a look at it.

It's the same code is the same code as earlier.

the original implementation.
The original is incremental implementation, but we also add the log message so that we know what's
going on.
And if we have a start date and an end that date then we will add extra conditions for this insert.
Okay.
One thing you need to know here is that if you remember, the review date wasn't really a date.
It was a time.
So a specific time down to the second.
And the same happens here with the start date and the end date.

That's actually tags.

### Overview of the Popular dbt Orchestration Tools and how to Choose the Right Tool {#overview-of-the-popular-dbt-orchestration-tools-an}

```
Let's assume that you got to a stage with your DBT project that you're ready to put it into production.
Now, in these situations, one of the first questions that arises is how to orchestrate it.
What you want to do is, at least with most DBT project, is to execute it on a schedule.
You want your models to be materialized, let's say every night right after midnight, once all the
data came in for the previous day.
You want your models to be materialized, your tests to be executed, your snapshots to be created,
