### Advanced Dagster: Using Partitions with Incremental Models {#advanced-dagster-using-partitions-with-incremental}

```
So welcome back everyone.
I am here with Georg Heiler and Alexander Milinkevich.
And in this session we will talk about Dexter and the more advanced applications of Dexter.
Both Alexander and Georg, they are real pros when it comes to Dexter.
And so I will let them do the rest, and I will let them introduce themselves.
So my name is Alexander and I'm a data engineer based systems.
So.
Georg, please.
Hi, I'm George.
I have many affiliations in the long history on data processing.
And currently I'm working on one hand in the telco sector with magenta.
And on the other side, I'm chairing several research projects with a processing very large scale data
sets.
And today, Alexander and I want to share with you some of our knowledge, in particular stuff that
we have written down in a blog post, which we'll share in the, um, courseware afterwards.
So no need to take screenshots.
When you want to work with data, uh, it's important that you understand how to connect the pieces.
Uh, Sheldon already told us that, you know, text and no data orchestrated is important to connect
the pieces or the steps of the data pipeline.
However, when the data sets grow larger and larger in size, you cannot always reprocess the whole
data set.
You want to work with an incremental model in DBT, for example.
What is an incremental model?
It is based on partitions of the data set, so data accumulates over time.
The gravity with data is having is only showcasing over time as it gets larger and larger.
And what you want to do is you don't want to reprocess it all the time from scratch, but process the
individual partitions.
So for example, in case of data, data like you can see here on the screen, you might get data every
day like this.
And assuming one day you receive bad data, you can easily reload the now refreshed and fixed data from
the source for this one specific partition and ensure that downstream DBT models and other data assets
update accordingly.
This allows you to construct high quality, meaning idempotent, but also scalable and resource efficient
data pipelines.
But partitions do not necessarily only come in simple shapes like we see here, where we are totally
discrete, disjunct pieces.
Sometimes we have actually.
Nested partitions that are mapped with a time horizon.
So for example here we have hourly data that is to be rolled up and reported later on a daily level
or maybe on a quarterly level.
Partitions do not only work in that initially showcased way, but they can also work in such a way,
and that is quite important.
Imagine you have two data sets, one from data source A and one from data source B, and let's say A
is delivering hourly data and B daily data.
You obviously would want to trigger the next data set or join operation only if both A and be fresh
and all the data that you would expect to be there for this particular point in time has arrived.
And with this type of partitioning, it is not only a possibility, but it becomes easy because by modeling
the data dependencies explicitly, you can make this graph of data assets work in your favor.
You can make the data orchestrator help you get your job done easier, quicker, and in higher quality.
Partitions do not only come in temporal ways, but you can also have multidimensional partitions.
This is a more advanced set of partitioning, but it can come useful for more complex use cases.
I think it's just good to know about the possibility and about an orchestrator natively supporting this.
Um.
This, I think, so far for the partitions.
That's the abstract concept of how you can make data pipelines high quality and really effective.
Now, how does this translate over into DBT?
In DBT there are incremental models.
So maybe we go right into the demo and look how this can can can work for real.
I have prepared a demo here.
And let's start by looking at one of DBT models you already know.
We will later look at the reviews model.
Um, but I hope to show you also one of the others what it looks like.
My page is not loading.
So I will stick to actually the review model.
You're already pretty much aware of what this is doing.
It's basically preparing the data for downstream analysis.
And the major change that needs to be added is actually quite small.
You just add this conditional statement.
But if a model is to be executed in an incremental mode, something else happens.
So instead of always running a model in natural mode here, that does not allow you to do backfills,
which means it does not allow you to create item ports and data pipelines, which means it doesn't allow
you to create high quality data pipelines.
You would have a specific subset of the data.
So here, for example, designated by the date period or time range on the review dates that you're
operating on.
And this is basically all what you need to change from the DB side to make DB partition aware.

this.

First of all, we have to define like what type of partitions we are looking at.
So remember what I showed at the start.
We have several styles of partitions assuming daily partitions here.
You also have to specify what is the initial or first point in time.
We're looking at a partition.

The event needs to know what data you would expect to have because otherwise it cannot give you, let's
say, a green light, but everything is all clear.

As you can see here we have a variable start date and end date which is referenced.
I think you're already aware of what a variable is from a concept perspective in DBT.

Um, and we basically make use of this concept of variables and just need to inject the right information
to DBT.
So to do this we need a translator because every table might hopefully it doesn't have, but might have
a different column where you need to inject the partition expression.
The actor does not intrinsically know which tables are at the hourly partition, or which one's daily
partition, and what is the column that needs to be partitioned on.

Furthermore, we need to make this asset that we pass from the graph partition aware.

and that the DB or DB actually pulls in the the data on your machine and writes out on some other remote
storage.

Why?
Why, maybe we change of this traditional way.
It gives you an end to end control of the pipeline and the capability for testing.
And these new databases like Duckdb, are very powerful.
That means for the largest, like 80% of your data, you probably don't need a distributed system that
is consuming a large amount of resources and needs a lot of, um, let's say fine tuning, or at least
costing you a lot if it's a cloud data warehouse.
So you're using something that is simple, is easier to operate for you, but also cheaper.
And because it is allowing you to scale down the whole data pipeline onto a single laptop, it's easier
to test and easier to debug if something goes wrong.

### An interview with the Data Utilization Head of the Vienna Insurance Group {#an-interview-with-the-data-utilization-head-of-the}

```
So.
Hi, everyone.
I'm here with Janakpur and he's the head of Data Governance and Data Utilization at Vienna Insurance
Group, one of the major insurance companies out there.
Janusz, welcome to the course as well.
Thanks for having me.
So, um, so we've heard a little bit earlier together with Janusz and his company, to, to introduce
DVT.
And Janusz has been kind enough to, to propose to share a little bit about the DVT, DVT use case and
how they introduced DVT and the general challenges and success stories and why and and all around that.
So, yeah, I suggest that we we go and jump right in.
So so Janusz, can you share a little bit about the company itself and your use case and also the like,
how your department with data is structured at big?
Yeah, sure.
Hi, everybody.
It's great to be here.
So Vienna Insurance Group is a large insurance conglomerate in the Central European region.
And my my company is very actively selling insurance in this region.
Um, we're so we're a composite insurance company.
Very, very strong in the home insurance business, for instance.
And, well, the insurance business itself is quite complex one from a data perspective.
So there are a lot of sales channels, a lot of products and many different lines of services.
Of course, our main important service and most important service is settling insurance claims.
And so from a data perspective, my team is a small data engineering and data governance team.
We own a mid-sized data platform that collects and and integrates data from over 20 different source
systems.
We focus on mainly contract customer and service related data, and within the company we refer to this
data platform as a business development data platform because the data sets created in this platform
are used by business domains to to build products and services.
And this means that we are not a reporting platform.
So our main purpose is not financial reporting to authorities or auditors.
So we don't have to do anything with that use case.
Our use case is that domains, business domains, test diagnosed their products and services based on
the data that we aggregate.
And they also train their their new machine learning models on those data sets that we create in this
platform.
Um, another important detail is that the team is, is a composition of engineers and data stewards
and of course our data engineers are busy with, with building and maintaining, um, data pipelines.
The data stewardship is also an important part of a team.
Data steward is a bridge function between the business and the engineering domain.
And the job of a data steward is to make sure that the business not only accesses but also understands
and trusts the data sets that we build.
And so you can think of artifacts like data, glossaries data catalogs, uh, processes for measuring
and improving data quality as, as the job of a data steward.
And we keep these two functions together in my team because from like a business perspective, it's,
it's very important that it's transparent and clear what the data means and whether it can be trusted
in the platform.
Okay.
And.
Can you share a little bit about your journey of how you ended up using DVT and what what, what what
the problem was that you were looking to find a solution for?
Yeah, absolutely.
So the platform we're talking about, we we took it over about a year ago and this was a legacy platform.
So it had been up and running for over ten years.
And there were great many pipelines and data marts already built in that platform.
And the legacy of those ten years were that there were quite a few data marts that were well documented
and well maintained, but there were many others where documentation and transparency of the pipeline
was very limited or completely missing.
And our main challenge as a team was to really understand and take ownership of the business logic implemented
in those pipelines.
And at the same time, we also had a challenge that demand for new data data marts were coming in steadily
and we had to find a new methodology to build, um, data marts faster in a more transparent way with
better testing and documentation.
In the last couple of years, there's been a great many buzz about the DB2 and the capabilities of DBT
in the in the data universe.
So we're very curious about what DVT can do for us and, and how we can how we can use this tool to,
to solve our problems.
So when you say that you that you wanted to take ownership, can you break this down for us a little
bit about what what ownership means?
Yeah, absolutely.
So ownership in this context means that when we publish data, business domains can ask many questions
about what that data means, whether they can trust that data, what a certain column or definition
means in order to answer those questions, really need to see a clear lineage of how that data element
element is created from the source through the transformation until publication.
And of course, if we have all this in just code, we can like read the code and analyze the code to
answer those questions.
But it's a very slow and tedious process.
And typically business domains don't have that much time to wait for answers.
So in order to be fast and responsive in the communication about our data sets, we wanted to to better
own the pipeline.
So basically know what sources we aggregate, what are the intermediate steps, whether we are working
on a fact table or whether we are just putting in dimensions and and being able to really pinpoint any
part of the pipeline and open up a discussion about that in a meaningful way with the business domain.
Thank you.
And so you decided to to give DVD a shot.
And have you considered any alternatives?
Uh, no, actually.
So since is really right now the main application, the modern data stack for transformations within
warehouse transformations, we definitely wanted to start with it and, and we were kind of opportunistic.
So if it works out, we're fine.
If not, then we're going to look for another one.
Oh yeah, yeah.
Actually I usually do the same.
I also just started working with a client and there we had to build the data transformation layer and
taking a look at the market, it still seems that DB2 is, is kind of the the to go or to go to technology.
Yeah.
Still today and as you as you know, DB2 has its own managed solution provided by DB2 labs but it's
an open source software as is everyone in the course could see.
Have you also considered to go with the managed solution or you were certain that the open source is
the way to go for you?
At the moment we are.
We are.
We are working with the open source solution and the reason for that is that our stack is basically
on premise.
So we're not a cloud company yet.
And it's I think it's it's probably coming in the way.
But at the moment we we went for the open source solution actually, we were really interested in in
in time so how we can kind of test and probably fail fast if needed with DVT.
So we didn't want to lose time with, with, with getting all the arrangements for for going to the
cloud with this with this tool.
And, and the team is fluent in command line tooling and Python.
So it was an obvious choice that we're going to try the the OSS version.
Yeah, it makes sense.
And yeah, you just mentioned time to market and fail early.
So can you share a little bit about your your general approach of how you validated the idea of how
you introduce it on a POC level?
So so what what actions did you take to make sure that you figure out if dbta is the right solution
for you?
Yeah, absolutely.
So actually being pressed for time doesn't mean that we shouldn't give time to experimentation and really
getting a proof that the solution works out for us.
So in our journey with DVT, we we purposely envisioned two stages.
So stage one was to make sure that the DVT is a good fit from a business perspective so we can really
gain transparency, um, faster implementation documentation that data lineage with the tool.
So whether it is a good fit for for our challenge to to be more transparent and flexible in our design
and maintenance of the pipelines and, and the second stage we envisioned was okay once we are convinced
that DVT can can do that, we we have to tackle other issues, more technical issues, how we are going
to fit DVT to our existing tech stack and how we're going to get work together with all the tools that
we have.
But that's more like a technical part.
And we didn't want to start with that because first we just make sure that it solves the business problem.
And yeah, so what we did was that we took your course, so we just wanted to get a hang of what happened,
how to use DVT.
And then we also worked together with you on a small POC to, to refactor one of the legacy datamarts
with it.
And we wanted to really test the full capability of this tool and try to see the rough edges where where
we really need some tuning for us, what features work out the best way and, and, and that was a successful
process.
So it took about three months to to rebuild with one of the existing data marts completely with DVT
and and it was a very convincing journey.
So we we found that that DVT can really do what it what it promises it can do.
And and and already in the pilot phase, we could see how much value it brings to the table that we
can break down complex pipelines in a modular way.
And how fast, for instance, data quality issues immediately surface when you when you get this this
this granularity of of of the pipeline.
Um, altogether this took about three months and then we were ready to, to go to the next stage and
really put it into production.
Yeah.
And.
So another thing I wanted to ask about this is, is the resources.
So you started with this kind of an iterative POC development and how much how much resource did this
take and what what functions of the company you had to interact with or who who did you need to pull
in to make this happen?
Yeah.
So the POC was mostly done by my team, so two engineers were working on that in that in that span of
about three months.
But we had to involve at points also the business side.
So the, the, the, this artifact we chose to, to, to work on the pilot was a large customer database.
So that's that's a database that integrates customer data from different source systems and it can tackle
the challenge that different systems handle different data elements of the customer domain differently.
And it needs to be aggregated, cleaned and and published in a unified way for a business.
And in that process, of course, that was not engineering only.
So we had to set up a few, few workshops with the business as well to, to, to get meaningful answers
about, about certain parts of the of the logic that we need to work on.
This was this was a legacy pipeline.
So there were actually for for every question there was an answer in the pipeline.
But we had to make sure that it was a good answer.
And that answer is still valid after a couple of years later of development phase.
But actually it was like, as I said, about three months.
And then basically this this data mart was up and running in DB and it could so it could build the same
thing as we built with other tools before.
And you'll be able to quite some functionality about about this.
And I remember in the proof of concept phase already so you already mentioned that some of the problems
that DB2 solved for you was like tracking lineage and make taking care of the data quality and also
documentation.
So can you just probably summarize what, what are the most useful features for you?
What are the features you you enjoy the most?
Yeah, absolutely.
So I think, uh, the modular modular development of data code or data pipeline code is a killer feature
of this tool.
So in case of a real life large complex pipelines that, that are built up of a couple hundred models,
it's it's something that that really brings a lot of value to the table.
Um, as I said, um, when you can really measure the impact of modular development is when, when there
are problems.
So when, when there is something the business doesn't understand, when there is a data quality issue
and, and, and the fact that you have a clear lineage and that your business logic is broken down into
digestible steps, it's really easy to spark up conversation about, okay, how we can solve the problem,
how we can, how we can do differently, what kind of tasks we need to implement to make sure it works.
And that feature that you no longer have a large stored procedure, but you have interconnected models.
That's, that's, that's well, that's great value and it's a great approach to, to building pipelines.
Um, from the more technical perspective, what really made my team happy was that for instance, so
DB2 really abstracts away the dependency management of the models and parallelization.
So that's something you don't have to worry about.
It's something you don't have to write your custom code for and it's this great ease.
So you just basically build up the logic and DB2 takes care of all the technicalities in the background.
So that's another feature that, that, that we, we appreciate a lot.
Um, as we are also involved in data stewardship and some governance related issues, the fact that
the lineage and the documentation are like first class citizens in the world, that was, that was a
great advantage.
So it's, um, I remember that moment when, when, when the data engineers were developing the, the
models actually had a discussion about how to write better documentation for those models.
And it's, it's a great moment in life when engineers discuss how they can put more value into their
documentation.
And it really makes it easier it really makes it easier to to govern the pipelines and actually bring
it to the business in a way that that that's understandable and trustable.
Um, yeah, probably.
One last thing I can mention is that once we were in the production and product production phase, it
turned out that the fact that DVT is basically a part of the Python ecosystem and it's basically a lightweight
tool, it made it very easy to to put it into production.
So in using containers, fitting it into our CI CD pipeline, it was really a surprise to me how fast
we could actually put it to work on the company's tech stack.
Yeah, I love this too, that it's basically a big package and it's just an executable so that that

### Introduction - Maslow's Pyramid of Data {#introduction-maslows-pyramid-of-data}

```
Now we are going to cover on a high level how it fits into the modern data stack and what makes it unique.
To really understand this topic, I'm going to stop myself from throwing around buzzwords.
Instead, we are going to take a step back to see the big picture and then break it down into structured
and understandable elements.
First, let me abstract away the nitty gritty details to rely on what we already know.
I'm sure you've come across Maslow's Hierarchy of needs.
Back in high school.
Now, I'm no psychologist, but this concept helps us understand the hierarchy of human motivation with
its five tier model.
So each person has a hierarchy of needs ranging from basic psychological safety needs, esteem needs,
and finally, self-actualization as a category.
Satisfied.
The next level in the hierarchy dominates, but you can't move up if you fail to meet lower level needs.
So if you jump to a late stage too fast, that is not a solid pyramid, so there is a high likelihood
of failure.
Now why are we talking about psychology, I get that.
Well, this concept is directly applicable to our topic, which is DBT and analytics engineering, which
will be which will be clear as we progress with the videos.
Our data hierarchy of needs looks something like this.
So the base of our pyramid will be all about capturing the relevant data.
Because undeniably, without data, we would not even talk about DBT or analytics engineering.
Once we've collected the data, we level up to the second stage where we clean it and build our integration
flows.
This step is all about transforming and mapping our raw data into a format that will be valuable for
our downstream purposes, such as analytics.
In the next stage, we will load this clean data into our data lake or data warehouse so that in the
following stage, we can implement business analytics and a reporting layer.
Now in this course our journey will end at this stage.
But if you feel adventurous, you are more than welcome to explore the final stages where ML and AI
use cases are handled.
These are the five stages of data evolution that every data driven organization must master to succeed.
Now let's remove the top of the pyramid, the AI and BI stages as we discussed, because they are not
relevant for us in this course.
However, the remaining three stages are extremely critical, and so I will break up the pyramid into
individual pieces so that we can use them to showcase a very, very typical workflow.
```

### The Data Maturity Model {#the-data-maturity-model}

```
Let's look at the base of our pyramid, which is data collection.
What I really mean by this is the extraction of data from different source systems.
So what kind of source systems, you may ask?
And well, my answer is that it depends.
If you do manufacturing, it might be data that is picked up by sensors.
Or if you do marketing your source might be Google Analytics and website clickstream logs.
You may have heard about hedge funds using stock market data or the healthcare companies use radiology
image data.
There are endless number of APIs, business systems, or transaction databases that can act as a source
system ready for us to capture data out of them.
Now, data can come in different formats and in different ways.
For example, a security camera placed in a store might upload the footage every night to a server at
once, but the CCTV will send real time video footage of a place.
Uninterrupted.
If you think about it, there can be multiple cameras placed to record different events in different
qualities.
We might even upload a picture instead of a video as well.
We call this the three V's of Big data variety, velocity, and volume.
When we design our data architecture, it is critical to think about the three vs to build reliable,
scalable and maintainable systems.
Now our collected data will be loaded into a staging area.
Not to forget that the extracted data is likely not be clean.
And so in this step we will need to focus on improving the data quality.
We might do different things.
It depends.
It is dependent on the use case of course, but the most frequent mistakes and inconsistencies that
make data dirty are duplicate data.
For example, a person gets recorded multiple times in a management system, or we have inconsistent
values that are logically associated like zip codes and addresses, basically.
Or a user left out a field and we ended up having missing data such as the last name.
There are some other common ones, like typing mistakes in a country, names or a month like December
is written off as 20 instead of 12.
The goal of the transform phase is converting data from its operational source format into a data warehouse
format.
So so to say.
Data integration.
This step is all about writing our transform data from the staging area to a target database, where
the analytics workloads will be performed.
We can do loading two ways by doing a refresh, aka rewriting the data completely, which will replace
our old data.
Or we can do an update where only the changes we apply to the source data will be added to the data
warehouse.
Let me wrap up what happened so far.
We outlined the data hierarchy of needs that we illustrated with Maslow's infamous pyramid.
The pyramid consisted of five stages that described how data is evolved at typical organizations.
Then we broke up the pyramid into individual pieces and explained how they connect to one another.
We saw that in case we skip a stage, we are most likely going to fail in our quest to extract value
out of our data.
However, in addition, we learned one of the most important topics of data engineering, which we call
ETL, which stands for extract, transform, and Load.
ETL is nothing more than the first three stages of our data hierarchy of needs.
```

### ETL and ELT {#etl-and-elt}

```
Now that you are familiar with the Data Maturity model, let's dive into ETL and Dldt procedures to
understand why ETL got adopted and became so widespread.
I'm going to start with a riddle.
What do you think?
How much did the one megabyte hard drive would have cost you back in 1967?
Well, the answer is $1 million.
Of course, like throughout the decades, the cost of storage and computation has have decreased, and
so did the way data engineers handle data integrations.
If you think about it, when storage is expensive, you will seriously consider what data you will persist
in your database.
Back then, data engineers faced a ton of issues.
We could not just scale up and down as we do today.
We had to work with single node databases that had computing constraints and etc. these on prem resources
were expensive due to perpetual licensing and more storage and compute resources were scarce.
It made perfect sense to do the transformations outside of the database in a staging area.
That's why ETL was the widespread and traditional approach.
It perfectly made sense to first do the extraction, aka the data collection, second, the transform
outside the database, and lastly, the load.
However, ETL is not all fun and shiny to just bring a few example issues out of many ETL.
As you may know, if there's a change in the schema, then your model is broken.
Or if you think about velocity data velocity, it changes over time.
And scaling is not exactly an easy task.
Or if you want to extract from another source, it takes time to add new data connections.
Not to mention the horror that comes with testing and debugging an ETL.
Now about ELT.
So the word has changed.
As you know, storage costs like $0.02 these days for every one GB of data.
So it was logical to reorganize the traditional ETL workflow.
Since today's computation and storage resources are super cheap, There is no harm in loading raw data
from source systems right into the destination.
Data warehouses like snowflake, redshift, and BigQuery are extremely scalable and performant, so
it makes sense to do the transformations inside the database rather than an external processing layer.
We can handle issues with upstream schemas and downstream data models that would have been rather rather
hard, uh, with the traditional ETL approach, where transformations were done in between the load
and the extraction stages.
Today for ELT, we have tools like Fivetran and stitch and many more that let us connect to source systems
and load data into our destination with just a few clicks.
Our lives are much easier with ELT as the standards, thanks to modern analytics, databases and cloud
computing.
And since extract and load L steps are done by Fivetran and stitch.
Let's talk about what what we have for the T aka the transform.
```

### Data Warehousing - a short introduction {#data-warehousing-a-short-introduction}

```
I'm sure you have heard of data warehouses as they have been around since the 1960s, and they are commonly
referred to as DW or DW is.
The purpose of a data warehouse is to serve as the technology for data analytics and reporting.
It's nothing more than a database that lets us do high performance, optimized analytics on our data
and with which we can create dimensions, facts, and handle denormalization.
These databases work really well with columnar storage formats and complex data types, but they can't
handle unstructured data such as images or videos.
Typically, you interact with a data warehouse by executing SQL against it, so data warehouse is nothing
more than a performant engine that lets us do analytics workloads on our data using SQL.
Chances are you use data warehouses for reporting or dashboarding purposes, so it's very important
to keep the data in the data warehouse well structured and clean or cleansed so that you don't need
to work days and weeks to answer very simple questions when your manager asks you something.
Back in the day.
Um, data warehouses were typically on premise based uh, provided by vendors such as IBM or Oracle,
just to name a few.
However, as cloud computing became available, your organization probably rolled out a cloud based
or cloud warehouse already, such as redshift, snowflake, or BigQuery, just to name a few.
There are huge trade offs between on premise and cloud based data warehouses.
If you have an on premise based data warehouse, you have complete control over your setup so you can
decide on the size of the storage you use, or the compute power and memory that you assign to your
workloads.
It comes in really handy if you have strict regulatory compliance compliance policies.
You control both the hardware and the software.
Of course, the total cost of ownership is huge as you need to invest a lot in him into maintenance.
So if hardware breaks down, you replace, you can replace it.
Plus, you need to hire different people like sysadmins and and network engineers.
On premise data warehouses will be harder to scale as you need to purchase new sets of computers and
and probably pass procurement processes.
Not to mention the headache that comes from with getting licenses from vendors and the upfront cost
that you need to spend on.
For example, electricity.
Now, of course, these are the reasons enterprises are shifting towards cloud based data warehouses
as they are very easy to maintain.
In fact, your cloud provider.
Cloud provider will probably do the heavy lifting.
So you will get transparent, predictable pricing and insane scalability.
It's super scalable as your cloud.
Cloud provider will assign you a leader node for which you can add or remove compute nodes as you wish.
This wraps up on a high level.
All you need to know about cloud and on prem warehouses.
So see you in the upcoming video.
```

### External Tables and Cloud Data Warehouses {#external-tables-and-cloud-data-warehouses}

```
As we covered.
You know now that there are trade offs, whichever way you go, whether you decide to build an on premise
or a cloud data warehouse.
But there are issues with both designs.
First of all, you pay for the compute nodes, whether they are used or they are not used.
And we are not talking about lethal amounts here.
In case you have large data sets, that's very pricey.
Even if your data warehouse storage is underutilized as you have small amounts of data, but it requires
high analytical workloads, you will face the material consequences.
Second, you can set up autoscaling, but it does not necessarily mean you can scale to meet your peak
workloads.
So what can we do?
Well, there is a there is this concept called external tables.
We have the option to store large files outside of the data warehouse in for example, Amazon S3 or
blob storages.
So we can decouple the compute component from the storage component.
At this point, we can scale the compute and the storage independently from the data warehouse instances.
You will not face any practical difference in querying when implementing this.
These external tables are on the cloud will be stored on serverless service whose infrastructure is
fully managed by your cloud provider.
So now we handle the storage and compute for structured data.
But what do we do in case we have unstructured data like images and videos, videos or text files?
Let's cover that in the next video.
```

### Data Lakes {#data-lakes}

```
Now we know what to do when storing structured data, therefore that we use the data warehouse.
But let's take the time to explore what happens when we have to deal with unstructured or semi-structured
data.
There is this concept of a data lake.
You can think of a data lake as a repository, where you can put all kinds of data ranging from raw,
uh, cleansed, unstructured, semi-structured, and so on.
It is like a very scalable file system on premise.
This will be called HDFS or Hadoop Distributed File System.
But for cloud there are others like Amazon S3 or Azure.
Azure Data Lake Storage Gen2 aka ADLs two.
The point of the data lake is to store files so they have no compute integrated.
This means that if your analytical workloads increase, you can scale your compute instances independently
from storage and your cloud provider will take care of handling external tables itself.
If you use Databricks or Snowflake, these providers will store your data in the data lake by default,
and they will only provide you with analytical clusters that you can set up the way you want.
So if you if your workloads are big, you will provision a bigger cluster and vice versa.
```

### Data Lakehouse {#data-lakehouse}

```
The Lakehouse concept emerged due to the limitations of data lakes.
It essentially combines the best features of data lakes and data warehouses.
In the case of a lakehouse, we have very similar data structure and data management features that we
have in our data warehouse.
However, it sits on top of a low cost cloud storage.
What's great in a in Lakehouse is the cost efficient storage provided by your cloud provider.
Also the asset transactional support so you can ensure consistency and that the schema of the data is
stored in a Lakehouse Metastore.
Additionally, you can evolve the schema of your tables without having to make a copy of the data set.
As for governance, you can control and authorize access to your data and will get an interface with
which you can connect to BI tools to the Lakehouse.
```

### The Modern Data Stack {#the-modern-data-stack}

```
In this video, we are going to cover what steps were taken throughout the years, or more precisely,
what improvements happened in the engineering world.
It opens the doors for the creation of DBT.
There were many limitations in the past with regards to the legacy or the traditional traditional data
stack, if you will.
So in this video, we are going to go step by step and cover the most critical changes, after which
you will understand in which direction data engineering and analytics engineering is going to work.
I hope having an understanding of the past will give you a better understanding of today's technologies,
and maybe the future as well.
So let's get started.
We are going to reiterate some of the concepts that we covered before, because they are important parts
when it comes to the modern data stack.
As you remember, storage prices back in the day were not exactly cheap.
I told you in a previous video that in 1967.
A megabyte of storage would have cost you around $1 million.
So when the traditional data integration process ETL, were introduced in the 1970s, data engineers
did not lead the transformation process before the load process.
As you remember, ETL was all about collecting your data from disparate source systems and loading them
into a centralized repository or data warehouse, if you will.
The transformations and normalization typically happen in the staging area before the load, where the
de-duplication of the data, the cleansing of the data were performed as storage prices were high.
This was an important step in reducing the amount of data that ultimately got loaded and kept inside
the data warehouse.
However, um.
Over the years, storage prices continued to drop along with the transistor prices, which are the majority
of components on a CPU or also declined.
So the transistor prices also declined, and we ended up with faster internet connection speeds so we
could transfer data faster and faster over the network.
These three events were critical as they had engineers rethink the processes that existed.
One of the most critical changes that started the shift from the legacy data stack to our today's modern
data stack happened to data warehouses, and it is closely, closely related to the internet speeds
from the previous slide, if you remember.
So if you focus your attention on the top left side of the slide, you will see a legacy SMP or symmetric
multiprocessing database design.
An SMP multiprocessor system is one in which many processors share resources, so single instances of
memory I, o devices, storage and the operating system are examples of these shared resources.
These resources are linked via a shared bus.
The processing occurs in parallel inside the CPU cores while the other system resources are shared.
An SMP system consists of a single physical computer with several CPU cores, as you can see on the
picture as well, the storage and the compute are tightly coupled, so when it comes to scalability,
SMP systems can only be scaled up or scaled vertically if you will.
This means that I can add more disks to a computer or more CPU.
However, the problem is that there are limitations as to how much I can fit into one machine.
The benefit of these SMP systems were that the processing happened on the same machine close to the
storage, which led to faster processing times.
This is a particularly good design when it comes to the time it takes to process data, as you have
everything in a single machine.
Yet the SMP architecture has its limitations, like it is restricted to only vertical scaling and the
need to keep backups of your data.
The reason why we could not store data in a distributed manner, like on multiple machines, was that
we simply did not have the distributed routing technologies that would have been required.
Thus, data transmission over the network was not exactly fast, if you remember.
However, since distributed systems and routing technologies evolved, and the data transmission speeds
over network got a lot better.
It opened the doors towards MPP or in other words, multi parallel processing database architectures.
These systems are made up of a master node and many compute nodes that do not share memory.
Each node is equipped with its own operating system and memory.
The master node is in charge of coordinating the operations among the compute nodes, and communicates
with them over high speed messaging interface.
Storage can be shared or not, so this is entirely up to you and the architecture is referred to be
shared storage or shared nothing.
Multi parallel processing architecture.
Shared nothing.
MPP is the most common MPP MVP design.
There is no difference across the systems.
Every node has its own memory and disk storage, and data is spread between the nodes depending on the
distribution mechanism employed.
Data from a single table might span many nodes, and each node only processes the data rows on its related
disks for it for processing.
To increase scalability, we may add more nodes which associate with associated memory and disk storage,
which is known as horizontal scaling or scaling out.
You would connect to a master node as a user or an application via JDBC or ODBC, and the master node
would coordinate the distribution process.
Our today's cloud data warehouses all have an underlying MPP architecture, such as.
As you also can see Azure Synapse, AWS redshift, BigQuery, or snowflake.
So the shift from SMB to MPP was a very critical step towards the modern data stack.
Another important step was the decoupling of storage and compute.
As with multiple processing architectures, the control or the master node can evaluate requests from
business or analytics applications and route them to A to computing nodes for processing.
If you look closely at this design, you can see that computing is only required when a request is made
by the business or analytical applications.
But storage is decoupled and may acquire data continuously without involving the compute.
So you can basically shut down the compute nodes when you don't need them.
And your data will be safe inside a low cost storage system such as Amazon S3 or Azure Blob storages.
It also lets you scale your compute very dynamically.
So if you have a very compute intensive workload, you can just add more compute to your cluster without
having to scale the storage with it as the storage reside outside of the worker nodes.
Another step that happened and really contributed to the modern data stack was the creation or introduction
of column oriented databases.
Row oriented databases, um, on the other hand, arrange data by record, so storing all of the data
associated with the record in memory Row oriented databases are the conventional technique of arranging
data, and they continue to offer some important advantages for storing data fast.
Typically, these row oriented data bases are designed to be efficient when it comes to writing rows
or reading rows.
So you would still want to use these row oriented databases in OLTP databases like Postgres or MySQL.
However, it turns out that row oriented databases are not exactly good for analytical workloads.
So column oriented databases were designed and introduced, and column oriented databases arrange data
by field, storing all the data associated with a field in memory.
Columnar databases have increased in popularity because they improve query performance, so they are
optimized for efficient column reading and computation.
If you look at the picture on the left side, you can see that if you want to sum up the number of cells
in a row oriented database, we would need to load the entire table.
Yet in a column store, we could just simply load the sales column without having to add, um, without
having to load the entire table.
And that is the beauty of column oriented databases.
They just require less input and output operations and can make the query times a lot faster.
So they are a great match for OLAp, uh based databases.
And our MPP cloud data warehouses today are all columnar.
Because of the MPP column oriented and decoupled cloud data warehouses.
Well, it's a massive, massive term.
We are now able to shift away from the ETL, extract, transform, load, data integration, traditional
processes to extract, load, transform, or ELT, as we discussed before in another video as well.
We have covered in the video previously, but what I want to highlight here is that these steps that
we discussed were inevitable throughout the years for us to be able to do ELT today.
So if you look at this picture, you can see an ELT implemented with tools that we have in the modern
data stack.
Fivetran and stitch are one of the most popular extract and load tools that we have available today.
The transformation layer sits on top of a cloud data warehouse and it uses DBT.
You have looker as a BI tool for example, and census for reverse ETL.
Now the modern data stack really is structured differently than traditional legacy tools for for example,
in a traditional data stack, your BI tool would not only do visualizations, but it would also act
as a data warehouse and would have storage integrated.
So you would have done everything in your BI tool, and these tools were massive and complicated.
So we can say that they were a group of layers that were vertically integrated, since they had storage,
since they acted as data warehouse, and you did visualizations in them as well.
But the modern data stack really flattens this out.
stout, and the modern data stack is a horizontally integrated set of tools that are fully managed,
that are in the cloud and that are cheap and easy to use.
And because corporations realize that data is a product of itself, we now see that DevOps tools are
crawling.
And not not just tools, but practices are crawling into this modern data set state space and are gaining
a lot of popularity.
We do the data engineering and analytics engineering engineering that follow software engineering and
DevOps best practices today.
So the modern data stack is just the productionization of the different layers of the data integration
flow.
DBT Productionize the T in the ELT.
They came up with DBT as a tool to do the transformations inside the data warehouse.
See you in the next video.
```

### The Basics of Slowly Changing Dimensions {#the-basics-of-slowly-changing-dimensions}

```
This section will introduce you to slowly changing dimensions QCD.
Think of SEDs as particular data that changes rarely and unpredictably, requiring a specific approach
to handle referential integrity when data is changed in the source database, how that change is reflected
in the corresponding data warehouse table decides what data is maintained for further accessibility
by the business.
In some cases, storing history data might not be worthwhile, as it might have become obsolete or entirely
useless.
Therefore, it can be simply thrown away.
But for some businesses, historic facts might remain relevant in the future, for example for historical
analysis.
Hence, simply erasing it would cause loss of valuable data.
There are a number of approaches to considering the data management and data warehousing of scds, called
the SCD types.
Some SCD types are used more frequently than others, and there is a reason for that.
In the following videos, we will walk through SCD type 0 to 3 and look at some benefits and drawbacks
they have.
```

### Type 0 - Retain Original {#type-0-retain-original}

```
Let's talk about SCD type zero.
We want to implement it if some data may not be worthwhile to maintain anymore for the business.
In the case of SCD type zero, the dimension change is only applied to the source table and is not transferred
to the data warehouse table.
For Airbnb, think of a scenario when a property owner changes his fax number he wants provided to Airbnb
when he first joined the platform.
Back in 2008, when Airbnb launched businesses, they still used fax numbers to some extent.
Hence, Airbnb gathered these fax numbers of its clients.
By 2010, faxing went entirely out of fashion.
Hence, there is no point for Airbnb to apply changes to fax data in its data warehouse anymore.
In this case, Airbnb will use SCD type zero and simply skip updating the fax data column in the data
warehouse table.
```

### Type 1 - Overwrite {#type-1-overwrite}

```
Let's talk about SCD type one.
In some cases when a dimension changes, only the new value could be important.
The reason for the change could be that the original data has become obsolete.
In these cases, we want to make sure that the new value is transferred to the data warehouse.
While there is no point in maintaining historical data in these scenarios, SCD type one will be our
choice, which consists of applying the same dimension change to the corresponding record in the data
warehouse as the change that was applied to the record in the source table.
When looking for accommodation on Airbnb, you can filter for accommodation with air conditioning and
property owners can provide whether they have air conditioning installed at their place.
Now imagine a situation when a property owner started marketing his flat on Airbnb a while ago, when
his flat didn't have air conditioning, but since then he installed or decided to install air conditioning
at his place to please his customers, and therefore he updated his information of its listing to show
that his place now has air conditioning installed.
For Airbnb, it is no longer relevant that the property did not used to have air conditioning.
It only matters that it does now, and so Airbnb will use SCD type one and apply the same data change
to the records in the source and the data warehouse table.
```

### Type 2 / SCD2 - Add New Row {#type-2-scd2-add-new-row}

```
There are also situations when both the current and the history data might be important for our business.
Besides the current value, history data might also be used for reporting or could be necessary to maintain
for future validation.
The first approach we will look at to tackling such a situation is SCD type two.
When a dimension change leads to an additional row being added to the data warehouse table for for the
new data, but the original or previous data is maintained.
So we have a whole overview of what happened.
The benefit of SCD type two is that all historical historical data is saved after each change, so all
historic data remains recoverable from the data warehouse.
Additional columns are added to each record in the data warehouse table to indicate the time range of
validity of the data, and to show whether the record contains the current data.
Consider rental pricing data for Airbnb.
Property owners may increase or decrease their rental prices whenever they wish.
Airbnb wants to perform detailed analysis on changes in, you know, the rental prices to understand
the market.
And so they must maintain all historical data on rental prices.
If a property owner changes the rental price of their flat on Airbnb.
Airbnb will store both the current and historic rental price.
In this case, using SCD type two can be a good choice for Airbnb as it will transfer the current data
to the data warehouse while also making sure historic data is maintained.
From SCD type two, it becomes less obvious which type to use.
So we will have to dive deeper into understanding what serves best purpose of what we are trying to
achieve.
The benefit of SCD type two is that all historic data is maintained, and that is, and that it remains
easily accessible.
But it also increases the amount of data stored.
In cases where the number of records is very high to begin with, using SCD type two might not be viable
as it might make processing speed unreasonably high.
```

### Type 3 - Add New Attribute {#type-3-add-new-attribute}

```
Let's talk about SCD type three.
There can be scenarios when keeping some history data is sufficient, for example if processing speed
is a concern.
In these cases, we can decide to do a trade off between not maintaining all history data for the sake
of keeping the number of records in our data warehouse table lower.
In case of SCD type, three columns instead of additional rows are used for recording the dimension
changes.
This type will not maintain historic values other than the original and the current values.
So if a dimension changes or it happens more than once, only the original and the current values will
be recoverable from the data warehouse.
When looking for an accommodation on Airbnb, you can choose between three different types of places
shared rooms, private rooms and entire places.
Now imagine a situation when a property owner started renting a room as a private room.
Let's say some years later, he decides to move out and to market his flat as an entire place.
Airbnb might want to do some analysis to see how properties have had their type changed since since
joining the platform, but they don't really care about the changes that are no longer valid.
So now let's say this person who now rents out his entire place, decides to move back and list the
place as a shared room.
In this case, we no longer keep history of the private room the way the place was listed originally.
We only care about the room type that came right before the current one.
In this case, we care about the fact that the entire place was listed.
Airbnb therefore can decide or will decide to use.
Step three adding additional columns to their data warehouse table to store both the original and the
current type of the property.
The benefit of type three is that it keeps the number of records lower in the data warehouse table,
which allows for more efficient processing.
On the other hand, it does not allow for maintaining of all the historical data and can make the data
more difficult to comprehend as it has specified.
Has to be specified which column contains the original and the current value.
```
